[2026-01-31T21:54:47Z] Sumit Gupta: The Legend of the Plow ↵ The most famous account of Cincinnatus takes place in 458 BC. Rome was facing a military crisis, surrounded by the Aequi and Sabines. In a state of emergency, the Roman Senate took the drastic step of appointing a Dictator—a magistrate with supreme authority for a six-month term to handle a specific crisis. ↵ According to legend: ↵ • The Call to Service: The Senate messengers found Cincinnatus on his small farm, literally in the middle of plowing his fields. ↵ • The Victory: He dropped his plow, donned his senatorial toga, and took command. Within just 15 days, he raised an army, defeated the invaders, and rescued the trapped Roman forces. ↵ • The Resignation: Despite having total control of Rome for another five and a half months, Cincinnatus immediately resigned his dictatorship and returned to his farm the moment the war was over. ↵ Why He Matters ↵ Cincinnatus became a legendary figure because he resisted the "corrupting" influence of power. In an era where leaders often fought to keep control, his willingness to go back to a simple life of manual labor made him a hero of the Roman Republic. ↵ His Legacy in Modern History ↵ • George Washington: Often called the "American Cincinnatus," Washington consciously modeled his behavior after the Roman leader by resigning his commission as Commander-in-Chief after the Revolutionary War and later stepping down after two terms as President. ↵ • The Society of the Cincinnati: A historical organization founded in 1783 by officers of the Continental Army to preserve the ideals of the Revolution. ↵ • Cincinnati, Ohio: The city was named in honor of the Society of the Cincinnati, forever linking the Roman farmer-general to the American Midwest.

[2026-02-01T08:22:20Z] Abhishek Vats: 2. In the mid-60s, artist X gave music legend Y a "Double Z" silkscreen painting (now worth tens of millions of dollars). Y reportedly didn't care much for it. He famously used it as a dartboard for a while before eventually trading it to his manager, Albert Grossman, for a used sofa. ↵ Recollecting the incident X said :“I liked Y, the way he created a brilliant new style… I even gave him one of my silver Z paintings in the days when he was first around. Later on, though, I got paranoid when I heard rumors that he had used the Z as a dart board up in the country. When I’d ask, ‘Why did he do that?’ I’d invariably get hearsay answers like ‘I hear he feels you destroyed Edie,’ or ‘Listen to ______ — I think you’re the ‘diplomat on the chrome horse, man.’ I didn’t know exactly what they meant by that — I never listened much to the words of songs — but I got the tenor of what people were saying — that Y didn’t like me, that he blamed me for Edie’s drugs.” Id X, Y and Z.
